Get on well : 
to have a good relationship or deal successfully with a situation. Synonyms : see eye-to-eye.
Example 1 : I get on well with my colleagues.
Example 2 : She gets on well with her neighbours and often invites them over for tea.
Example 3 : Even though they have different personalities, the two teammates get on well and work effectively together.
Employ :
to have someone work ( to serv ) or do a job for you and pay them for it.
Example 1 : By 1960 the arms industry in America directly employed 3.5 million people.
Example 2 : The company has employed many skilled workers over the past year.
Example 3 : A large part of the workforce is employed in agriculture.
Example 4 : I want to employ him because he is clever and intelligent.
Engage :
1 - having formally agreed to marry.
2 - to interest someone in something and keep them thinking about it.
3 - to become involved, or have contact, with someone or something.
4 - VERB + ENGAGE : attempt to, seek to, try to
Example 1 : He was engaged in agriculture when he was a teenager.
Example 2 : She likes to engage directly with the anime.
Example 3: They have been engaged for two years.
Example 4 : I just got engaged.
Example 5 : The brothel is the place where people pay to engage in sexual activity.
Bother :
To concern, to trouble, to disturb.
Example 1 : Why are you bothering me?
Example 2 : I had a little bother finding your house.
Example 3 : I wouldn't go to the bother of making the cakes myself.
Understandable :
Easy to understand ( internalize, grasp, and perceive).
Example 1 : Your sentences is completely understandable.
Example 2 : His speech is perfectly understandable.
Validate :
to make something officially acceptable or approved.  Synonyms : To approve, to prove, to confirm, to verify, to certify, to assert.
Example 1 : This book was validated by Cambridge University. (The sentence needs a verb in the passive voice (because the book is being validated by someone).
Example 2 : Everyone is waiting for the judge to validate a decision.
Example 3 : The professor validated the students’ arguments with strong evidence.
Acceptable :
Satisfactory (Synonym) and able to agreed to or approved of.
Example 1 : Yogurt is a perfectly acceptable substitute for cream in cooking.
Example 2 : a compromise that is acceptable to both sides.
Example 3 : We must come up with a solution that our shareholders will find acceptable.
Example 4 : Your sentence is completely acceptable.
To look out :
to watch what is happening and be careful.
Example 1 : I thought something had happened in outside and I looked out.
Example 2 : My mother looks out for me to make sure I go home safely.
Example 3 : Look out the window and see who's at the door.
Paragraph :
While crossing the street, I had to look out for cars speeding by. My friend wasn’t paying attention, so I told him to look out and be careful. “You always look out for me,” he said with a smile, and I replied, “That’s what friends are for!”
Forged :
To make illegal copy of something in order to deceive.
Example 1 : The passenger gave the police forged insurance, so they took her to prison.
Example 2 : Through years of hard work, she forged a successful career in the tech industry. (Tech is a short form of technology.)
Example 3 : The employee was fired after they discovered his forged signature on the contract.
Example 4 : Over the years, they forged (created) a strong friendship based on trust and mutual respect.
Forge :
1 - To form by a mechanical or hydraulic press with or without heat.
2 - Create or Build (literal or figurative).
3 - Make something fake (illegal use).
Example 1 : The blacksmith will forge a new sword from iron.
Example 2 : It's a crime to forge official documents like passports or ID cards.
Example 3 : He forged a strong friendship with his colleagues through trust and teamwork.
Example 4 : A good friend is someone who tries to forge a great relationship based on trust and respect.
Deceive : [De:siv]
To persuade someone that something false is the truth. Synonyms : to cheat, to fool someone.
Example 1 : He promised to me, but he didn't keep his word and deceived me.
Example 2 : He gave his friend false information to deceive him.
Example 3 : Deceiving people isn't the right behaviour.
Example 4 : Human nature is such that we easily deceive ourselves.

To leave (Synonym) a place.
Example 1 : I asked him to go away because I needed some time to think.
Example 2 : I hope this headache will go away after I take some medicine.
Example 3 : We decided to go away for the weekend to relax by the beach.

Example 2 : Words that have three or more syllables.
Example 3 : She split the word up into syllables so that he could have a go at spelling it.
Assemble :
To come together. Synonym :  to attend, to accompany, to join, to follow.
Example 1 : We decided to assemble before going to the football match.
Example 2 : He will have a birthday party tomorrow and everyone is excited to assemble together.
Example 3 : Today is a very special day for him, and I think we can assemble to make something for him.
Transcend :
to go further, rise above, or be more important or better than something, especially a limit.
Example 1 : Great leaders often transcend personal interests to focus on the greater good of their community.
Example 2 : She worked hard to transcend the limitations placed on (assigned to) her by society and achieve her dreams.
Example 3 : Art has the power to transcend cultural and language barriers, connecting people on a deeper level.

Example 2 : She doesn't want to help me and it drives me crazy.
Example 3 : She doesn't pay attention the car speeding by. It drives me crazy.

the sound of people clapping their hands repeatedly to show enjoyment or approval (confirmation) of something such as a performance or speech.

Example 2 : He decided to buy travel insurance before going on his international trip.

Even when mistakes were made, she believed it was crucial to acknowledge them, learn from them, and move forward with a positive mindset.

Shell :
1 - The hard outer covering of certain animals (like snails, turtles, or clams).
2 - The outer covering of nuts, seeds, or eggs.
3 - A structure that looks like a protective outer case.
4 - A hollow container filled with explosive material (used in military context).
5 - As a verb: to remove the shell from something (like peas or nuts) or to bombard with shells. 
Literal meanings (animal shell, nut shell): Very common.
Figurative/military meanings: Common, depending on context (literature, war, idioms).
Example 1 : The turtle pulled its head into its shell.

Example 3 : Twelve gauges. You need shells?

Example 5 : They shelled the peas for dinner.

With the new system in place, I hoped to set up a routine that would help me stay focused and efficient.

The forecast predicted heavy rain throughout the day, and the chance of it stopping seemed unlikely. However, the team was determined to make it work, adjusting the schedule and preparing for the worst. They knew it was unlikely they could change the weather, but they could still ensure that everything else was ready, no matter the conditions.

Remainder : 
the part of something that is left after the other parts have gone, been used, or been taken away.

Example 2 : She ate most of the cake and left the remainder for later.
Example 3 : The store sold most of the books, and the remainder will be donated to charity.

	ü Appreciate means to value, enjoy, or be grateful for something.

to breathe in a very noisy way while you are sleeping.

Example 2 : His snores kept me awake all night.
Approximately [Apuroksimetly] :
close to a particular number or time although not exactly that number or time. Synonyms : Partly, roughly, around, about, nearly.
Example 1 : The trip will take approximately three hours.
Example 2 : There were approximately 500 people at the concert.
Example 3 : The building is approximately 100 years old.
Enthusiasm [Intusuazim] :
a feeling of energetic interest in a particular subject or activity and an eagerness to be involved in it. Synonyms : desire, passionate.
Example 1 : His enthusiasm for coding is so contagious that everyone around him gets excited to learn something new!
Example 2 : She approached every programming challenge with enthusiasm, turning even the toughest bugs into opportunities for growth.
Example 3 : With great enthusiasm, they launched their first Java application, eager to see how users would react!
Contagious : [Kənteiges]
A contagious disease can be caught by touching someone who has the disease or by touching an infected object, or by an infected person coughing, sneezing, etc. near you.
Example 1 : A contagious disease spreads quickly from person to person.
Example 2 : Her laughter was so contagious that soon, the whole room was filled with people laughing along with her.
Example 3 : His passion for learning was contagious, inspiring his friends to study harder and explore new subjects.

1 - Moving in a direction on or beside something. (As a Preposition)
2 - Together with someone or something. (As a Adverb)
Example 1 : There are trees along the road.
Example 2 : Sing along with me!
Example 3 : She brought her dog along to the park.

Different from.
Example 1 : Unlike his usual quiet self, today he spoke with great enthusiasm about his new programming project.
Example 2 : Unlike other students, she approached challenges with contagious excitement, turning every obstacle into an opportunity to learn.

to show, point, or make clear in another way.
Example 1 : The map indicates the nearest gas station.
Example 2 : His smile indicated that he was happy.
Example 3 : A high fever may indicate an infection.

having no doubt or knowing exactly that something is true. Synonyms : Absolute, sure, definitely, accurate, proper

Auxiliary [aqziliyeri] :
giving help or support.  Synonyms : deputy, maid, servant, ancillary.
Example 1 : In English grammar, auxiliary verbs like "do," "be," and "have" help form different tenses and questions.
Example 2 : The hospital set up an auxiliary power system to ensure electricity during emergencies.
Deputy :
1 - A person who is appointed to act as a second-in-command or assistant, often taking charge when the leader is absent.
2 - An official with authority, especially in law enforcement (like a deputy sheriff).
3 - A member of parliament or a legislative body in some countries.
This word is common, especially in politics, business, and law enforcement contexts.
Example 1 : She works as the deputy director of the company.
Example 2 : The sheriff and his deputies arrested the suspect.
Example 3 : The deputy spoke in parliament about the new law.

Prevalent : 
existing very commonly or happening often. Synonyms : broad, wide, spacious.
Example 1 : These diseases are more prevalent among young children.
Example 2 : Trees are dying in areas where acid rain is most prevalent.
Example 3 : The use of smartphones is prevalent in today's society, with almost everyone relying on them for communication and information.
Example 4 : A sense of enthusiasm was prevalent among the students as (during this time that) they prepared for the coding competition.
As :
1 - Shows the reason for something.
2 - Describes the role or function of someone/something.
3 - Used in comparisons to say something is similar to something else.
4 - Used in formal or poetic expressions.
5 - Shows that two actions are happening at the same time.
Example 1 : As I was leaving, she arrived. 
Example 2 : As he studied he listened to music.
Example 3 : I didn’t go to the party as I was feeling sick.
Example 4 : I used my phone as a flashlight.
Example 5 : She sings as beautifully as a bird.
Example 6 : Tired as he was, he kept working.
Example 7 : Child as she is, she speaks wisely.

the way in which you are treated and educated when young, especially by your parents, especially in relation to the effect that this has on how you behave and make moral decisions.
Example 1 : Her kind and respectful behaviour reflects her excellent upbringing.
Example 2 : Despite his strict upbringing, he developed a very independent mindset.

strongly limiting someone's freedom to behave as they wish, or likely to severely punish someone if they do not obey.

the raising of children and all the responsibilities and activities these are involved in it.

Roundabout :
1)A circular intersection or junction in a road where traffic flows around a central island, often with no traffic lights. Vehicles enter and exit the circle at different points. (Noun)
2)Indirect or not straightforward. When someone is being roundabout, they are not being direct in their communication, often giving more complex or indirect explanations. (Adjective)
Example 1 : The roundabout near the park can be confusing for new drivers.
Example 2 : His roundabout way of answering the question made it hard to understand his opinion.
Example 3 : She explained the problem in a roundabout way, making it harder to understand.

Upon taking her seat, she sighed in relief, knowing she wouldn’t miss her important meeting.

Some students also accused him because they saw him looking at another paper. However, after checking carefully, the teacher realized Tom was just looking around because he was nervous. Smuggle :
To take things or people to or from a place secretly and often illegally.
Example 1 : The police caught the gang smuggling rare animals across the border.
Example 2 : They tried to smuggle gold out of the country hidden in their bags.
Example 3 : The criminals planned to smuggle drugs across the river at night.
Example 4 : They planned the smuggling operation carefully to avoid getting caught.
Example 5 : She helped smuggle important documents during the war.
Paragraph :
Smuggling has been a problem for centuries, affecting countries all around the world. People often smuggle goods like drugs, weapons, or rare animals to avoid taxes or laws. Smuggling can be very dangerous, not only for the smugglers but also for society. It harms the economy, spreads illegal products, and sometimes even supports organized crime. Many governments work hard to stop smuggling by using strict laws and border controls.
Morally :
Based on principles that you or people in general consider to be right, honest, or acceptable.
Example 1 : It is morally wrong to cheat on a test.
Example 2 : She felt morally responsible for helping the poor.
Example 3 : They made a decision that was morally right, even though it was difficult.
Frostbite :
Injury to someone caused by severe cold, usually to their toes, fingers, ears, or nose, that causes permanent loss of tissue.
Example 1 : He got frostbite on his fingers after walking for hours in the snow without gloves.
Example 2 : Frostbite can cause permanent damage if it is not treated quickly.
Example 3 : The hikers had to stop and warm up before frostbite set in.
Paragraph :
When you stay outside in freezing temperatures for too long, you risk getting frostbite. Frostbite happens when the skin and tissues freeze because of the extreme cold. It usually affects parts of the body like the fingers, toes, nose, and ears. At first, the skin may feel very cold and numb, but if you don't warm up quickly, serious damage can happen. People with frostbite may see their skin turn white or blue, and in severe cases, they might even lose feeling in that part of the body. That’s why it’s important to dress warmly and protect yourself when the weather is very cold.
Tissue :
1 - A group of connected cells in an animal or plant that are similar to each other, have the same purpose, and form the stated part of the animal or plant.

Notice : [noutis]
To observe or become aware, not to say something.

Example 4 : The teacher noticed that I forgot my homework again.
Paragraph :
As I walked into the classroom, I noticed that the lights were off and everyone was quiet. I quickly noticed a sign on the board that said, “Test Today.” I didn’t notice the date earlier, so I wasn’t ready. I sat down nervously, hoping the teacher wouldn’t notice how unprepared I was.
Faulty :
A faulty machine or device is not perfectly made or does not work correctly.
Example 1 : I believe the item is faulty and I would like to return it or receive a replacement.
Example 2 : The phone I bought last week is faulty because the screen doesn’t turn on.
Example 3 : We returned the faulty laptop and asked for a new one.
Paragraph :
Last month, I bought a new microwave from an online store. When it arrived, I was very excited to use it. However, after a few days, I noticed it was faulty. The buttons did not work well, and it stopped heating food. I called customer service and told them about the faulty product. They asked me to send pictures and then agreed to replace it. Now, I am waiting for the new one to arrive.
Severe :
causing very great pain, difficulty, worry (anxious), damage, etc.; very serious.
Example 1 : The region is experiencing a severe drought that has destroyed most of the crops.
Example 2 : She suffered a severe injury during the accident and had to undergo surgery.
Example 3 : The teacher gave him a severe warning after catching him cheating on the exam.
Paragraph :
The storm last night caused severe damage across the town. Trees were uprooted, power lines were knocked down, and several homes lost their roofs. Emergency services worked through the night to assist those affected by the severe weather conditions. Many families had to evacuate and seek shelter in community centers. The local authorities warned that another severe storm might be approaching, urging everyone to stay alert and prepared.
Undergo :
to experience something that is unpleasant or something that involves a change.
Example 1 : He underwent a severe operation.
Example 2 : We underwent serious training for this project.
Example 3 : The company underwent major changes in recent months.
Paragraph :
He underwent stick training to get into university. During this time, he faced both physical and mental challenges. During the training period, he underwent intense training programs and failed many tests. But this process was very important to improve himself. Undergoing such an experience is a big step for his future. 
Permanent :
Lasting for a long time or for ever.
Example 1 : She got a permanent tattoo of her favourite quote on her wrist.
Example 2 : He wants to live in this city permanently.
Example 3 : This decision will have a permanent effect.
Paragraph : 
I have always wanted to have a permanent job. A temporary job is useful, but it doesn’t give me security. A permanent job means that I can make plans for the future and feel more stable in my life.
Constant :
Means something that happens continuously or regularly over time.
Example 1 : This area has constant rain.
Example 2 : His constant lateness disturbs the teacher.
Example 3 : The constant sound of the phone makes me tired.
Paragraph :
My neighbour’s dog constantly barks every night, and it disturbs my sleep. Because of constant noise, I feel tired every morning. I'm trying to get used to it now, but constant disturbance sometimes drives me crazy.
Admission :
Refers to the process of being allowed into a place, institution, or accepting the truth about something.
Example 1 : She received admission to the university.
Example 2 : He has already submitted his documents for admission to the university.
Example 3 : His admission of guilt surprised everyone.
Paragraph :
Last week, we went to the museum with our family. Admission was very cheap — only 3 manats. There were interesting exhibitions and ancient artifacts in the museum. We were required to show our ticket at the entrance. This visit was both fun and educational.
Competitive : 
Wanting very much to win (to acquire, to gain) or be more successful than other people.
Example 1 : I am a competitive person, and if I want to achieve something, I will put in a lot of effort.
Example 2 : He is an absolutely competitive person and always tries to be the best.
Example 3 : Working in a competitive environment is motivating for me.
Example 4 : This company offers competitive prices in the market.
Paragraph :
For me, competitiveness is a key to achieving success. I have always worked hard to be the best and reach my goals since I was a child. A competitive environment gives me more strength and motivation because I enjoy improving myself and testing my limits.
Passion :
A very powerful feeling, for example of sexual attraction, love, hate, anger, or other emotion.
Example 1 : Her passion for helping others inspired her to pursue a career in social work.
Example 2 : His passion for music started in his childhood.
Example 3 : Painting isn't just a job; it's a passion.
Example 4 : He inspired thousands of children with his passion for education.
Paragraph :
A key to success is having a passion for your job. If you do your job with passion, you never feel like it’s an obligation.
For example, if a teacher approaches education with passion, they don’t just teach lessons — they also inspire their students. Enthusiasm helps people work without feeling overwhelmed and encourages them to keep learning and improving themselves. That’s why it’s important to consider your passion when choosing your path in life.
Obligation :
A duty or commitment that a person is bound to carry out, often due to law, contract, or moral principle.
Example 1 : If you're passionate about your job, working doesn't feel like an obligation.
Example 2 : Delivering this project on time is our main obligation.
Example 3 : According to the contract, he has an obligation to present a report monthly.
Paragraph :
Every person has specific obligations to the community. These include obeying the law, paying taxes, and protecting the environment. When people fulfil these obligations, the community becomes more stable and fair.
Institution : [instətuşın]
A large and important organization, such as a university or bank.
Example 1 : Harvard University is a prestigious institution known for its academic excellence.
Example 2 : This educational institution is one of the best universities in the country.
Example 3 : The government plans to create a new scientific institution.
Paragraph :
There are many influential institutions in Azerbaijan. These institutions operate in the fields of education, science, and technology, conducting important research. Working at these institutions is a valuable experience and a great opportunity for young specialists to grow.
Passionate : [pəşineyt]
having very strong feelings or emotions. Synonyms : Enthusiasm, desire, excited.
Example 1 : I am passionate about learning new things and continuously improving myself.
Example 2 : I am passionate about technology and enjoy staying up to date with the latest innovations in software development.
Example 3 : As a team member, I’m passionate about collaboration and believe great results come from shared ideas and effort.
Prestigious : [prestiges]
Respect and admiration given to someone or something, usually because of a reputation for high quality, success, or social influence.
Example 1 : This university is known as a prestigious institution with an influential environment.
Example 2 : He was offered a position at a prestigious tech company after completing his internship.
Example 3 : The scientist received a prestigious award for her groundbreaking work in renewable energy.
Example 4 :  His article was published in a prestigious scientific journal.
Paragraph :
I have always wished to study at a prestigious university. I have been passionate about technology and science ever since I was a teenager. During my studies, I participated in several projects, and these experiences have taught me many valuable lessons. After graduating from university, I would like to work at one of the most prestigious workplaces in the country. My goal is to keep improving constantly and to become a well-known professional in the future.
Contract :
a legal document that states and explains a formal agreement between two different people or groups, or the agreement itself.
Example 1 : Azerbaijan and Armenia signed a contract to formalize everything in legal documents.
Example 2 : The two companies signed a contract to jointly develop a new software application.
Example 3 : She carefully reviewed the terms of the contract before agreeing to the job offer.
Paragraph :
Two companies signed a contract to implement a new project. According to the contract, both parties must fulfill their obligations and are responsible for completing the project on time. If one party breaches the terms of the contract, the other party can take legal action. The contract also outlines financial matters and terms of cooperation.
Influential :
Having a lot of influence on someone or something.
Example 1 : I always wished to study at one of the most influential universities in the country.
Example 2 : This teacher is known as an influential person in the field of science.
Example 3 : The book which he wrote became influential among the young generation.
Paragraph : 
Many teenagers in Azerbaijan wish to study abroad. They choose influential universities for quality education and greater opportunities. These experiences play an important role in their future careers. Studying abroad is also a chance to learn about new cultures and broaden their worldview.
Groundbreaking :
If something is groundbreaking, it is very new and a big change from other things of its type. Synonyms : innovative.
Example 1 : A groundbreaking invention like the internet changed the world.
Example 2 : He discovered a groundbreaking method for the treatment of cancer.
Example 3 : This groundbreaking technology will completely change the industry.
Example 4 : His research in the medical field led to groundbreaking discoveries.
Paragraph :
In recent years, groundbreaking discoveries have been made in the field of science. Especially, research conducted in the field of biotechnology has created valuable opportunities for human health. These groundbreaking technologies enable more effective treatments with fewer side effects on the human body.
Rely :
to need a particular thing or the help and support of someone or something in order to continue, to work correctly, or to succeed. Synonyms : to believe, to trust.
Example 1 : I rely on my best friend whenever I need help.
Example 2 : I can rely on my family all the time.
Example 3 : You must rely on your team to achieve success.
Paragraph :
I have always tried to rely on my friends throughout my life. Their support has been very important to me. Sometimes, a person can't rely on their own strength, so relying of the support of others is also important. Especially, family and close friends play a supportive role in our lives.
Formalize :
To make something official or decide to arrange it according to a fixed structure.
Example 1 : They decided to formalize their business agreement with a contract.
Example 2 :  They signed a contract to formalize their cooperation.
Example 3 :  We have to formalize these rules so that everyone can follow them.
Paragraph :
The team working on the project came up with some good ideas. However, to implement them, it's important to formalize them. The management only looks at officially presented plans and makes decisions based on them. That’s why the team is planning to formalize their ideas this week.
Jointly :
It is used to describe something that is done together by two or more people or groups. Often used in legal, business, or official contexts. 
Example 1 : The two companies jointly developed the new software.
Example 2 : This project was jointly funded by two companies.
Example 3 : The decision was jointly made by the director and the manager.
Paragraph :
Two companies jointly made a decision to work on the development of new technology. They jointly financed the project and shared the responsibilities. The contract was jointly signed by both parties.
Implement :
To start using a plan or system. Synonyms : carry out, execute, apply.
Example 1 : Two companies jointly agreed to implement a new software application.
Example 2 : The teacher successfully implemented the lesson plan.
Example 3 : The government implemented actions to enforce the new laws.
Paragraph :
Two companies jointly started a cooperation to implement digital transformation strategies. They chose new software and implemented it across all departments. During this process, training was provided to the employees, and additional actions were taken to ensure the system worked efficiently. The project was successfully implemented, and the results were better than expected.
Worldwide :
Existing or happening in all parts of the world.
Synonyms : all over the world, across the globe, throughout the world.
Very common, used in business, media, and general communication.
Example 1 :The company delivers its products worldwide.
Example 2 : The film has gained worldwide fame.

Meanwhile : 
1 - An adverb meaning “at the same time” or “during the same period.”
2 - It can also mean “in contrast” or “on the other hand” when comparing two situations.
Very common, especially in both spoken and written English.
Example 1 : She was cooking dinner; meanwhile, her husband was setting the table.
Example 2 : I was preparing the food; meanwhile, he was writing a letter.
Example 3 : He was in the meeting; meanwhile, I was waiting for him at home.
Example 4 : I was writing the code; meanwhile, she was teaching English to children.
Enforce :
1 - To make people obey a law, rule, or regulation.
2 - To ensure that a decision, agreement, or standard is carried out or followed.
Synonyms : implement, apply, uphold.
Very common, especially in legal, governmental, and organizational contexts.
Example 1 : The police enforce traffic laws strictly.
Example 2 : Teachers strictly enforce the rules at school.
Example 3 : The government strictly enforce the new taxes.

Breach :
1 - (Noun) An act of breaking a law, rule, or agreement.
2 - (Noun) A gap, break, or opening in a wall, barrier, or defense.
3 - (Verb) To break through or violate something, such as a rule or barrier.
Synonyms : violate.
Common, especially in legal, military, and formal contexts.
Example 1 : The company was fined for a breach of contract.
Example 2 : The company faced heavy fines because it breached environmental regulations.
Example 3 : Employees who breached confidentiality agreements might be fired.

1 - (Adjective) Of high quality or satisfactory.
2 - (Adjective) Thin, delicate, or precise.

1 - The state of keeping information secret or private.
2 - The duty or obligation to protect sensitive information from unauthorized access.
Common, especially in legal, medical, and business contexts.
Example 1 : Employees must adhere to confidentiality regulations.
Example 2 : The government must protect people's confidentiality.
Outline :
1 - (noun) A general plan or summary that shows the main points of something without details. (Common)
2 - (noun) The outer edge or shape of an object. (Less Common)
3 - (noun) A short written or spoken description giving the main ideas. (Common)
4 - (verb) To describe the main points or basic structure of something. (Common)
Common, both in daily language and academic/professional contexts.
Example 1 : The teacher asked us to prepare an outline for the essay.
Example 2 : The manager has requested the project's outline.
Example 3 : The outline of the mountain was visible against the sky.
Example 4 : I need to give an outline of what happened yesterday.
Example 5 : She gave a brief outline of the company’s future plans.
Example 6 : The document outlines marital property rules.
Conduct :
1 - (Verb) To organize, direct, or carry out an activity (for example, conduct an experiment, conduct a meeting).
2 - (Verb) To guide or lead (for example, a tour guide conducts tourists).
3 - (Noun) A person’s behavior or way of acting.
4 - (Verb, technical) To allow something (like heat, electricity) to pass through.
Very common, in formal, academic, technical, and everyday contexts.
Example 1 : The police will conduct an investigation into the case.
Example 2 : The teacher's conduct was disrespectful toward the children.
Example 3 : The company will conduct (hold) a meeting to discuss the results.
Example 4 : Copper conducts electricity very well.
Example 5 : The manager’s conduct was questioned by the board.
Foal :
A baby horse.
Not very common in everyday English; mostly used in farming, horse breeding, or animal-related contexts.
Example 1 : The foal stayed close to its mother in the field.
Confine :
1 - (Verb) To keep someone or something within limits; restrict.
2 - (Verb) To keep someone in a place they cannot leave (like prison, hospital, or room).
3 - (Noun, plural “confines”) The boundaries or limits of a place or area.
Synonyms : restrain, restrict.
Confine suggests keeping something or someone within a specific, often narrow, boundary. The focus is on a physical or spatial limitation.
Fairly common, especially in formal, medical, or legal English.
Example 1 : We should not confine our imagination to only one solution.
Example 2 : Everyone was confined to their house during Covid-19.
Example 3 : The illness confined him to bed for two weeks.
Example 4 : The refugees lived within the narrow confines of the camp.
Example 5 : Working remotely is definitely hard because you have to confine yourself to stay at home without seeing anyone.
Survey :
1 - (Noun) A method of collecting information by asking questions to people.
2 - (Noun) A general examination or overview of a subject or area.
3 - (Noun/Verb) The process of measuring and mapping land.
4 - (Verb) To look carefully at something in detail.
Very common, in research, business, education, and construction contexts.
Example 1 : The company will hold a survey of employers.
Example 2 : The book gives a survey of European history.
Example 3 : Engineers surveyed the land before construction began.
Example 4 : She surveyed the room before making her decision.
Example 5 : I have to survey my project before releasing it.
Indoor :
1 - Happening, used, or located inside a building, not outside.
2 - Related to activities, sports, or places that take place under a roof.
Antonyms : Outdoor.
Common, especially when distinguishing between inside and outside activities.
Example 1 : Smoking is not allowed in indoor areas.
Example 2 : I don't like staying indoors because I prefer to see the beauty of traveling.
Example 3 : The hotel has an indoor swimming pool.
Example 4 : I don't like staying outdoors because I prefer to stay at home and just improve myself toward my purposes.
Nonstop :
1 - Without stopping or pausing; continuous.
2 - (Adjective) Refers to something happening without breaks or interruptions (like travel, work, or speech).
3 - (Adverb) Describes doing something continuously.
Very common, in everyday English, especially in travel, work, and casual speech.
Example 1 : The teacher is speaking nonstop and seems exhausted.
Example 2 : We took a nonstop flight from Baku to London.
Example 3 : She worked nonstop to finish the project.
Example 4 : The baby on the upper floor cried nonstop all night.
Harsh :
1 - Very severe, cruel, or unkind in behavior or judgment.
2 - Unpleasantly rough, strong, or extreme in effect (like weather, sound, or light).
3 - Too strict or demanding.
Synonyms : abrasive, cruel, unkind, severe.
Very common, in both daily and formal English.
Example 1 : The teacher gave a harsh punishment to the students.
Example 2 : The driver who breached the traffic law was fined harshly by the police.
Example 3 : The light was too harsh for my eyes.
Example 4 : The teacher's harsh words hurt his feelings.
Gaze :
1 - (Verb) To look steadily and intently at something or someone, often with admiration, wonder, or deep thought.
2 - (Noun) A long, steady look.
Fairly common, but more often used in literary, poetic, or descriptive language than in casual speech.
Synonyms : stare (Sometimes could be negative.)
Example 1 : She gazed at the stars in silence.
Example 2 : The child gazed out of the window, lost in thought.
Example 3 : Everyone gazed at the artist with the excellent performance.
Example 4 : His gaze was fixed on the painting.
Prone :
1 - Likely or having a tendency to do something (usually something negative).
2 - Vulnerable or susceptible to something.
3 - (Adjective – position) Lying flat, especially face downward.
Common, especially in formal, medical, and everyday contexts when talking about tendencies or risks.
"prone to" is a phrase that is typically followed by a gerund (the "-ing" form of a verb used as a noun).
Example 1 : He is prone to getting headaches when stressed.
Example 2 : He is prone to making mistakes writing code.
Example 3 : You start to be prone to using AI every day.
Example 4 : The soldier lay prone on the ground.
Liable :
1 - Legally responsible for something.
2 - Likely to do or experience something; at risk of.
3 - (Formal) Exposed or subject to a negative outcome.
Common, especially in legal, financial, and formal English.
Example 1 : You are liable to catch a cold if you don’t wear a jacket in the rain.
Example 2 : You are liable to finish your task on time.
Example 3 : Drivers are liable to heavy fines if they break the law.
Example 4 : The company is liable for any damage caused by its products.
Example 5 : He is liable to make mistakes if he doesn’t concentrate.
Fatigue :
1 - Extreme tiredness, usually from physical or mental effort.
2 - (Medical) A condition of constant weariness that reduces a person’s ability to function.
3 - (Military, less common) Manual work or duty, such as cleaning or building, assigned to soldiers.
4 - (Engineering) Weakness in materials caused by repeated stress or use.
Common, in medical, daily, and technical contexts.
Example 1 : After the long journey, she suffered from fatigue.
Example 2 : After the harsh project, I started to be prone to fatigue.
Example 3 : Mental fatigue made it hard for him to focus.
Example 4 : When I broke up with my girlfriend, I found myself with mental fatigue.
Example 5 : The bridge collapsed due to metal fatigue.
Sarcastic : 
1 - Using irony or mocking words to hurt, criticize, or show contempt.
2 - Speaking in a way that seems humorous but is meant to wound or ridicule.
3 - A tone or remark that sounds insincere and cutting.
Very common, especially in daily conversations, literature, and media.
Example 1 : He made a sarcastic comment about her cooking.
Example 2 : He often makes sarcastic comments, and it caused them to break up.
Example 3 : His sarcastic tone made it clear he wasn’t really praising me.
Example 4 : The teacher used some sarcastic words unwillingly.
Sting :
1 - (Verb) To pierce or wound with a sharp point (like an insect or plant).
2 - (Verb) To cause a sharp pain or burning sensation.
3 - (Verb) To hurt someone emotionally with words or actions.
4 - (Noun) The wound or pain caused by a stinger.
5 - (Noun, informal) A police operation to catch criminals by tricking them.
Common, in everyday speech, medical, and law enforcement contexts.
Example 1 : The bee stung him on the arm.
Example 2 : My eyes sting from the smoke.

Vulnerable : [valnırıbıl]
1 - Easily hurt or harmed, physically or emotionally.
2 - Open to attack or damage, not well protected.
3 - Susceptible to certain conditions or risks.

Example 1 : Children are vulnerable to certain diseases.
Example 2 : I am vulnerable to watching scary movies.
Example 3 : The apartment was vulnerable to the earthquake.

Susceptible : [səsəptıbl]

2 - Easily influenced by feelings or emotions; sensitive.
3 - (Less common) Capable of being affected or treated in a certain way.
Common, especially in medical, psychological, and formal contexts.
Example 1 : Young children are susceptible to colds.

Example 3 : Someone who falls for compliments easily is susceptible to flattery.
Assert :
1 - To state something firmly and confidently.
2 - To declare one’s rights or authority strongly.
3 - To make others recognize or respect your position, rights, or opinions. 
Common, especially in formal, legal, academic, and professional contexts.

Forcefully :
1 - In a way that is strong, powerful, or with great energy.
2 - In a way that shows firmness, determination, or conviction.
Common, especially in formal writing, debates, and descriptions of physical actions.

Unweariedly : [Anviridly]
Without becoming tired, exhausted, or discouraged; with constant energy and persistence.
It is uncommon in modern everyday English. You’ll see it more in literature, poetry, or older texts rather than daily speech.

Boast : [boust]
1 - (Verb) To talk with pride about something you have or achieved, sometimes in an exaggerated way.
2 - (Verb) To possess something remarkable or impressive.
3 - (Noun) A statement of pride about oneself or something owned.
Synonyms : brag, talk big, gloat.
Common, in both everyday and formal English.
The verb boast is often followed by the preposition about when you are talking about the thing that someone is boasting about.
Example 1 : She gave him a sarcastic smile when he boasted.
Example 2 : I don't like people who boast about how easy something is to achieve.
Example 3 : He boasted about winning the competition.
Example 4 : The hotel boasts a beautiful view of the sea.
Scrum :
1 - In Rugby (sports): A method of restarting play where players from each team bind closely together and push against the opposing team to gain possession of the ball.
2 - In Project Management (especially Agile): A framework for managing and completing complex projects, particularly in software development. It emphasizes teamwork, iterative progress, and continuous improvement.
In sports → Mostly common in countries where rugby is popular (UK, Australia, New Zealand, South Africa, etc.).
In project management → Very common worldwide, especially in IT and software development.
Example 1 : The referee called for a scrum after the ball went out of play.
Example 2 : Our team holds a daily scrum meeting to discuss progress and obstacles.
Example 3 : The players made a scrum to discuss the next move.
Example 4 : During the Daily Scrum, our team members briefly discussed their progress and any obstacles.

						
Excited :
Feeling very happy, enthusiastic, or eager about something.
Very common in everyday English, both spoken and written.

Example 2 : The children are excited about going on vacation.
Example 3 : I am excited to reveal my project which I have been working on for 3 years.

Sled :
A small vehicle with runners (instead of wheels) used for traveling over snow or ice, usually pulled by animals or ridden downhill.
Common in regions with snow; less common in everyday English for people living in warmer climates.

Example 5 : My head was aching after a long day of sledding.
Example 6 : The kids went down the hill on a sled.

Evidence :

Deprivation :

2 - Lack or denial of basic needs, such as food, sleep, or comfort.
Synonyms : lack, shortage.
Common in medical, psychological, social, and formal contexts.
Example 1 : Sleep deprivation can affect your health.
Example 2 : He complained about the deprivation of his rights.
Example 3 : Many people suffer from food deprivation.
Example 4 : Many children suffer from social deprivation.
Accusation : [əkyuzeşın]
A statement saying that someone has done something wrong, illegal, or bad.
Common in everyday English, especially in legal, political, and formal contexts.
Example 1 : The journalist made a serious accusation against the politician.
Example 2 : The lawyer proved the accusations step-by-step.
Example 3 : The judge overturned the accusation due to a violation of the regulations.
Example 4 : The police made an accusation that he committed the robbery.
Sore :
1 - Adjective – Painful, aching, or tender (physically or emotionally).
2 - Noun – A spot on the body that is painful, injured, or infected.
Very common in both spoken and written English.
Example 1 : My legs are sore after running.
Example 2 : Don’t touch that cut, it’s still sore.
Example 3 : She’s still sore about losing the competition.
Example 4 : My throat is sore after drinking fizzy drinks.
Erase :
1 - To remove something completely, especially writing, marks, or memories.
2 - To eliminate or destroy.
Very common in daily life, education, technology (like erasing files), and figurative speech.
Example 1 : He erased the mistake from the blackboard.
Example 2 : I need to erase embarrassing moments from my mind.
Example 3 : He erased his opponent with a high score.
Example 4 : The teacher told me to erase the incorrect answer and write the right one.
Example 5 : I didn't like the story, so I decided to erase everything and start over.
Internship :
A period of work experience offered by an organization for a limited time, often to students or recent graduates, to gain practical knowledge in a specific field.
Common – especially in educational and professional contexts.
Example 1 : She completed a three-month internship at a law firm.
Example 2 : You will struggle to find a job if you have never had an internship.
Example 3 : An internship is a great way to improve your communication skills.
Substandard :
Below the usual or required standard; of poor quality compared to what is expected.
Not very common in daily conversations, but commonly used in formal, academic, or professional contexts (e.g., quality control, law, reports).
Example 1 : The building was closed because of substandard safety conditions.
Example 2 : Some houses in Africa are built in substandard conditions.
Convenient :
Suitable or easy to use; fitting well with one’s needs, plans, or comfort.
Very common – used widely in everyday English, especially when talking about comfort, suitability, or ease of use.
Example 1 : This hotel is in a convenient location near the airport.
Example 2 : You can find multiple convenient supermarkets in Japan.
Example 3 : I'm a student and I'm seeking a convenient house.
Example 4 : Online shopping is very convenient for busy people.
Example 5 : It would be more convenient for me to meet you tomorrow.
Slipper :
A comfortable, soft, lightweight shoe, usually worn indoors.
Common – used often in everyday English, especially in the context of home clothing/footwear.
Example 1 : He put on his slippers after coming home from work.
Example 2 : She bought a new pair of fluffy slippers for winter.
Example 3 : I find it very annoying when my mother wears slippers because she makes a noise. 
Refund :
Money that is returned to a customer because they paid for something that was faulty, not delivered, or no longer wanted. It can also be used as a verb meaning to give money back.
Very common – especially in shopping, business, and online purchases.
Example 1 : I received a full refund for the shoes I returned. (noun)
Example 2 : The company refused to refund my payment. (verb)
Example 3 : I received a refund from the company because my products were late.
Example 4 : To make it up, I feel entitled to a full refund due to the inconvenience.
Example 5 : I would like to receive a full refund because my products are defective and broken.
Mandatory :
1 - Required by law, rule, or authority; not optional.
2 - Compulsory, obligatory.
Very common in legal, official, workplace, and educational contexts.
Synonyms : compulsory, obligatory.
Example 1 : Attendance at the meeting is mandatory for all employees.
Example 2 : Following traffic rules is mandatory for every driver.
Example 3 : Stealing money from a bank carries a mandatory punishment.
Example 4 : Wearing seat belts is mandatory in some countries, and I think it's a great way to get people to follow the rules.
Famine :
1 - A severe shortage of food in a large region, leading to widespread hunger and death.
2 - Figurative: An extreme scarcity of something (not only food).
Common in history, news, geography, and humanitarian contexts. Also used metaphorically for scarcity.
Famine is a widespread, societal event, while starvation is an individual's physical condition.
Example 1 : The country suffered a terrible famine in the 19th century.
Example 2 : According to the investigation, many countries had a famine after the world war 2.
Example 3 : There is a famine of new ideas in the industry.
Deprive :
1 - To take something away from someone, especially something necessary or important.
2 - To prevent someone from having or enjoying something.
Common in formal writing, law, politics, and also in everyday speech when talking about rights, opportunities, or needs.
Example 1 : The new law deprived citizens of their freedom.
Example 2 : My mom deprived me of playing games.
Example 3 : You're gonna deprive her of it, and you're telling me you're sorry?
Example 4 : It isn't true that the child is being deprived of his father's love.
Tend to :
1 - To be likely to behave in a particular way or to have a certain characteristic.
2 - To take care of, look after, or manage something or someone (less common).
Very common in spoken and written English; used to describe habits, tendencies, or care.
Example 1 : Things tend to get lost when you move house.
Example 2 : He tends to be late for meetings.
Example 3 : I tend to get stressed when I go to the board.
Example 4 : I tend to stay up late on weekends.
Ache : 
A continuous or prolonged dull pain in a part of the body. It can also be used figuratively to describe emotional pain or longing.
Common – “ache” is frequently used in everyday English, both for physical and emotional contexts.
Example 1 : My head aches after staring at the screen for hours.
Example 2 : My muscles ache after yesterday’s workout.
Example 3 : My shoulders ache after raising heavy things.
Example 4 : She ached for her hometown while living abroad.
Example 5 : Her fingers ached while playing the piano.
Setback :
An unexpected problem, delay, or difficulty that slows progress or makes success harder to achieve.
Common, especially in professional, academic, and personal growth contexts.
Example 1 : The project suffered a major setback due to technical issues.
Example 2 : I always face some setbacks when I try to create a new project.
Example 3 : The site is stopped due to a technical setback.
Example 4 : Don't let one setback stop you from taking advantage of an opportunity.
Emphasize : [emfəsayz]
1 - To give special importance or attention to something; to stress.
2 - Emphasis [emfesiz] -> Special importance, value, or attention given to something; stress placed on a word or idea.
3 - Emphatic [emfətik] -> Expressed or performed with emphasis; forceful and definite.
Very common in education, communication, writing, and speech.
Example 1 : The teacher placed emphasis on grammar during the lesson.
Example 2 : He emphasized the importance of punctuality.
Example 3 : She gave an emphatic reply.
Example 4 : The teacher's speech is emphatic.
Example 5 : I always watch videos on Youtube for improving my English and they often emphasize the importance of speaking.
Example 6 : He's putting his emphasis on his contract now.
Swollen :
Enlarged, puffed up, or distended, usually because of injury, infection, or fluid buildup.
Yes, it is a common word in everyday English, especially in medical or casual descriptions of health and nature.
Example 1 : My eyes are swollen from crying all night.
Example 2 : My hand is swollen after the bee stung me.
Example 3 : His face is all purple and swollen up with bruises.
Example 4 : After the long run, my legs are swollen.
Example 5 : You have to take him to the doctor. Otherwise, his legs could be swollen.
Substantiate : [subistenşiyet]
1 - To provide evidence or proof that something is true.
2 - To confirm, support, or validate a claim, statement, or argument.
Common in formal, academic, legal, and professional contexts; less common in everyday casual speech.
Example 1 : The scientist substantiated her theory with detailed experiments.
Example 2 : The lawyer substantiated the prisoner's claim with strong evidence.
Example 3 : He could not substantiate his accusations.
Example 4 : Do you have any evidence to substantiate your claim?
Commute :
1 - To travel regularly between one’s home and place of work or study.
2 - In legal use: to reduce a punishment to a less severe one.
Very common in daily English, especially in work-related contexts.
Example 1 : She commutes to the city every day by train.
Example 2 : I commute to the university every day by bus.
Example 3 : The governor decided to commute his prison sentence to community service.
Example 4 : My daily commute takes about 45 minutes.
Comfy :
Informal/short form of comfortable; meaning providing physical ease, relaxation, or coziness.
Very common in casual, spoken English.
Synonyms : Comfortable.
You should use "comfortable" in more formal or professional situations.
Example 1 : These slippers are really comfy.
Example 2 : This couch is super comfy.
Example 3 : I like to wear comfy clothes.
Theft : [feft]
1 - The act of stealing; taking someone else’s property without permission and with no intention of returning it.
2 - A crime involving unlawful taking of possessions.

1 - Causing an unpleasant feeling of fear, unease, or discomfort.

Very common in everyday English, especially in casual speech.

To take someone away illegally by force, often to demand money (ransom) or to gain some advantage.

Arch :

Sequence : [Sikvens]

Pan :
1 - A metal container used for cooking food (e.g., frying pan, saucepan).
2 - (Informal) In photography or film, a horizontal movement of a camera.
3 - To move a camera from side to side in a continuous motion.
4 - To criticize harshly (especially in reviews, e.g., “The movie was panned by critics”).
Common, especially the noun (cooking sense). The verb (“to pan” meaning criticize or move camera) is less common but still widely used.

Lid :
1 - A cover that fits tightly over a container, box, or pot to close or protect what’s inside. (Very Common)

3 - (Informal, mainly American) A hat or cap. (Not Common)

Example 2 : The lid of the box was made of metal. 
Example 3 : I left the lid up for you, though.
Example 4 : Close the toilet lid when leaving.
Example 5 : If you'd gotten it to the mainland, you could have blown the lid off this place.
Example 6 : We must keep a lid on costs this year.
						

Heighten : 
1 - To increase the degree or intensity of something — such as emotion, effect, or awareness. It makes something stronger, more noticeable, or more extreme. (Common)
2 - To raise or make higher physically — though this meaning is less common than the first. (Less Common)
Example 1 : The movie’s music heightened the feeling of suspense.
Example 2 : Hanging out with him just heightened my anger.

Example 4 : We embedded a new system that heightens people's security.

1 - To affect someone very strongly, often emotionally — to the point that they find it hard to think or react normally. (Very Common)
2 - To defeat or overpower completely, especially by force, number, or pressure. (Common)
3 - To cover or flood something completely, often with water or other physical force. (Less Common)

Example 4 : I felt overwhelmed by all the work I had to do.
Example 5 : He was overwhelmed by the kindness of his friends.

Prioritize : [Payroritayz]
1 - To decide which tasks or things are most important and do them first. (Very common (especially in work or study contexts)
2 - To give special importance to something. (Common (used both in daily and formal English)
Prefer is about liking something more than another.
Prioritize is about acting on that preference by assigning importance.
Example 1 : Young people should prioritize their school over games.
Example 2 : The government should prioritize education and healthcare.
Example 3 : When I create a project, I tend to prioritize building out the front-end and back-end at the same time.

2 - A man attending an event without a female companion (informal).
Noun (male deer): Common in wildlife or literature.
Noun (alone at a party): Common in informal speech.
Example 1 : A stag was grazing in the meadow.
Example 2 : He went to the party stag because his wife was busy.
Devotion :
1 - Strong love, loyalty, or dedication to a person, cause, or activity.
2 - Religious worship or profound dedication to spiritual practices.
Yes, very common in everyday, professional, and religious contexts.
Example 1 : She showed great devotion to her family.
Example 2 : His devotion to his work impressed everyone.

1 - A tournament or competition, especially in sports or games.
2 - Historically, a series of medieval contests or jousts.
Moderately common; mostly used in formal, historical, or sporting contexts.
Example 1 : The chess tourney attracted players from all over the country.
Example 2 : Knights competed in a grand tourney during the festival.

2 - To exaggerate or embellish something.

Example 2 : He tends to overdo his compliments.

Embellish :
1 - To make something more attractive by adding decorative details.
2 - To enhance a story or statement by adding extra details, often exaggerating.
Yes, moderately common in both literal (decoration) and figurative (storytelling) contexts.
Example 1 : She embellished her dress with sequins.
Example 2 : He embellished the story to make it more exciting.

Rake :
1 - (Noun) A gardening tool with a long handle and teeth, used for collecting leaves, hay, or soil.
2 - (Verb) To gather, move, or smooth something with a rake.
3 - (Noun, figurative) A man who behaves irresponsibly or immorally (less common).
Noun/Verb (gardening): Very common.
Figurative noun (man): Less common, mostly in literature.
Example 1 : He used a rake to collect fallen leaves in the yard.
Example 2 : She raked the soil before planting flowers.

Dent :
1 - (Noun) A small hollow or depression in a surface, usually caused by impact.
2 - (Verb) To make a small hollow or depression in something by hitting or pressing.
3 - (Figurative, Noun) A reduction or decrease in something (e.g., confidence, profits).
Yes, very common in everyday, automotive, and figurative contexts.
Example 1 : There was a dent in the car door after the accident.
Example 2 : He accidentally dented the table with his elbow.
Example 3 : The unexpected expenses made a dent in their savings.

2 - (Verb, informal) To push or nudge someone using the elbow.

Example 1 : He injured his elbow while playing basketball.
Example 2 : She elbowed her way through the crowded room.

2 - (Noun) A gentle push or encouragement.
Yes, very common in everyday speech and informal contexts.
Example 1 : She nudged him to wake him up.
Example 2 : He gave the dog a gentle nudge to move along.

Yes, common in formal writing, business, engineering, and everyday contexts.

Procure :
1 - To obtain something, especially with effort, care, or special means.
2 - (Formal use) To bring about or cause something to happen.
3 - (Old/Legal use) To persuade or arrange for someone else to do something.
Not very common in daily spoken English.
More common in formal, legal, or historical texts.
Example 1 : She managed to procure a rare book for her research.
Example 2 : The company procured supplies from different countries.
Upstart :
1 - (Noun) A person who has risen suddenly to wealth, power, or importance and is often regarded as arrogant or presumptuous.
2 - (Adjective) Describes someone or something that is new and has risen quickly, often seen as arrogant, inexperienced, or not fully deserving.
Moderately common in literature, history, and formal descriptions.
Less used in everyday conversation, but you may still hear it in business or politics.
Example 1 : Many nobles looked down on the young upstart who gained favor with the king.
Example 2 :
Overreach :
1 - (Verb) To try to do more than you are able to; to overestimate your abilities or power.
2 - (Verb) To stretch or extend too far, beyond proper limits.
3 - (Noun) An act of going too far, either physically or metaphorically. 
Moderately common in formal writing, business, politics, and history.
Less used in casual daily speech.
Example 1 : The ambitious general overreached and lost the battle.
Example 2 : His latest project was an overreach that drained company resources.
Example 3 : The athlete overreached during training and injured himself.
Charlatan :
1 - A person who pretends to have knowledge, skill, or special ability, but actually does not; a fraud or impostor.
2 - Someone who deceives others by claiming expertise in something they are ignorant of.
Not very common in everyday speech.
More common in formal writing, journalism, and when criticizing fake experts.
Example 1 : The so-called “miracle doctor” turned out to be a charlatan.
Example 2 : He exposed the fortune teller as a charlatan who tricked people for money.
Margin :
1 - The blank space at the edge of a page, notebook, or document.
2 - The difference between the cost of something and its selling price (profit margin).
3 - A small amount of freedom, allowance, or room for error.
Very common, especially in education, printing, business, and economics.
Example 1 : She wrote a note in the margin of the textbook.
Example 2 : The store operates with a 15% profit margin.
Example 3 : There is little margin for error in this risky plan.

Gut :
1 - (Noun) Stomach or belly; internal organs, especially the intestines.
2 - (Noun, informal) Courage, determination, or instinct (“having the guts to do something”).
3 - (Verb) To remove the insides of an animal or fish.
4 - (Adjective, informal) Based on instinct rather than analysis (“gut feeling”).
Quite common in both everyday and figurative speech.
Example 1 : The fish must be cleaned and gutted before cooking.
Example 2 : He had the guts to stand up to his boss.
Example 3 : She trusted her gut feeling about the decision.
Example 4 : The boxer took a hard punch in the gut.

Vengeance :
(Noun) Punishment inflicted in return for a wrong or injury; the act of taking revenge.
Somewhat common, but more formal and dramatic than “revenge.” Often used in literature, films, and formal speech.
Example 1 : Somewhat common, but more formal and dramatic than “revenge.” Often used in literature, films, and formal speech.
Stockade :
1 - (Noun) A defensive barrier or enclosure made of strong wooden posts or stakes driven into the ground.
2 - (Noun, U.S. military) A place where military prisoners are kept; a military jail.
Not very common in everyday English. More often seen in historical, military, or literary contexts.
Example 1 : The soldiers built a stockade around their camp for protection.
Example 2 : The prisoners were confined in the stockade.
Scroll :
1 - (Noun) A roll of parchment, paper, or other material used for writing, often historical.
2 - (Verb) To move text or images on a screen up, down, or sideways.
3 - (Noun, decorative) A design or ornament resembling a rolled-up strip or spiral.
Noun (historical scroll): Less common today, mainly in history or literature.
Verb (digital scrolling): Very common in everyday life.
Decorative noun: Moderately common in art and design contexts.
Example 1 : The ancient manuscript was written on a scroll.
Example 2 : She scrolled through her social media feed.
Example 3 : The artist carved intricate scrolls on the furniture.

Stangle :
1 - (Verb) To kill or injure someone by squeezing their throat and cutting off their air supply.
2 - (Verb) To suppress, restrict, or prevent something from developing freely.
Literal sense: moderately common in literature, news, or crime contexts.
Figurative sense: fairly common in business, politics, or social commentary.
Example 1 : The villain tried to strangle the hero in the story.
Example 2 : Excessive regulations can strangle innovation in business.

Crib :

2 - (Noun – Informal, US) A house or apartment (slang, especially in hip-hop culture).
3 - (Verb – Cheat/Copy) To cheat by copying someone else’s work or answers.
4 - (Noun – Informal, UK) A note or sheet used secretly in an exam (cheat sheet).
Very common in sense (1) for baby’s bed.
Common in slang (2) for “house” in American culture.
Academic “crib” (3,4) is less common but still used.

Plow :
1 - (Noun – Farming tool) A large farming tool or machine used to turn over the soil before planting crops.
2 - (Verb – Farming) To dig into and turn over soil with a plow.
3 - (Verb – Move forcefully) To move with great force through something, often pushing obstacles aside.
4 - (Verb – Work hard) To continue working hard at something, even when it’s difficult.
5 - (Noun – Astronomy) A star constellation shape (The Plough = Big Dipper in British English).
Very common in farming and rural contexts.
Figurative uses (“plow through work,” “plow through traffic”) are common in everyday English.
Astronomical sense is mainly British English.
Example 1 : The farmer used a plow to prepare the field.
Example 2 : They need to plow the land before planting wheat.
Example 3 : The truck plowed through the snow.
Example 4 : She had to plow through hundreds of pages to finish the research.

Discord :

Common in formal English, literature, and news when describing conflicts or disagreements.
Less common in everyday casual speech, where people more often say "conflict" or "argument."
Very common in music terminology.
Example 1 : There was growing discord between the two political parties.
Example 2 : Family discord can affect children deeply.

Crescent :
1 - (Shape) A curved shape that is wider in the middle and tapers at both ends, like a part of a circle.
2 - (Moon Phase) The shape of the moon when less than half of it is visible, either waxing (growing) or waning (shrinking).
3 - (Architecture / Streets) A curved street, often lined with houses, called a “crescent.”
Very common when talking about the moon.
Common in design, jewelry, and symbols (especially Islamic art and flags).
Less common in street/architecture usage but still used in English-speaking countries.
Example 1 : The crescent moon hung low in the night sky.
Example 2 : She wore a necklace shaped like a crescent.

Common in design, art, and fireworks descriptions.

Less common in everyday speech outside of those contexts.

Wage :
1 - (Noun) The regular payment a worker receives (usually weekly, daily, or hourly) for their labor or services.
2 - (Verb, formal/literary) To carry out or engage in (often used with "war," "battle," or "campaign").
Noun use: Very common in everyday English, especially in work, economy, and labor contexts.
Verb use: Less common in daily conversation, but very common in formal, historical, political, and literary contexts.

Multitude :
1 - A very large number of people or things.
2 - (Often with the) The general public; the masses of ordinary people.
Fairly common, but mostly in formal, literary, or descriptive contexts.
In daily casual English, people more often say “a lot of”, “many”, or “tons of” instead of multitude.
Example 1 : A multitude of stars lit up the night sky.
Example 2 : He faced a multitude of problems after moving to the city.

Converse :
1 - (verb) To talk, have a conversation with someone.
2 - (noun, formal) Conversation.
3 - (noun, logic/math) The reverse of a statement (in logic). Example: The converse of “If it rains, the ground gets wet” is “If the ground is wet, then it rained.”
4 - (adjective, formal) Opposite, reverse. 
Verb (to converse): Rare in casual speech (people usually say “talk” or “chat”). Common in formal/written English.
Noun (converse as conversation): Very rare, old-fashioned.
Noun/Adjective (opposite): Still used in formal, academic, or logical contexts.
Example 1 : We sat by the fire and conversed late into the night.
Example 2 : She enjoys intelligent converse with her friends. (rare, old-fashioned)
Cistern :
1 - A tank or container that holds water, especially for domestic use.
2 - In architecture/engineering: a reservoir for storing liquids, usually water.
3 - In anatomy (rare): a fluid-filled sac in the body (e.g., cisterna magna in the brain).
Common in British English (especially for the toilet water tank).
Less common in American English, where people usually just say “toilet tank” or “water tank.”
In academic/scientific contexts, “cistern” is also used (biology, medicine, history, architecture).
Example 1 : Rainwater collected in the roof flows into the cistern.
Example 2 : The toilet cistern needs repairing.

2 - To regard or treat with contempt; to reject with scorn. (verb)
Fairly common in formal or literary English.
Less common in casual everyday speech, where people might use “contempt,” “disrespect,” or “looked down on.”
Example 1 : She looked at him with disdain after his rude comment. (noun)

Suckle :
1 - She looked at him with disdain after his rude comment. (noun)
2 - To nurse at the breast or udder. (verb)
3 - A baby or animal that is still nursing. (noun, rare/archaic)
Not very common in everyday modern English.
Mostly used in medical, biological, or literary contexts.
In daily life, people usually say “breastfeed” instead of “suckle.”

Dodge :

2 - To avoid answering, doing, or facing something directly. (verb)

Common in everyday English, especially in sports, action contexts, or when someone avoids responsibility.
Also well known because of the American car brand “Dodge.”
Example 1 : He dodged the ball just in time. (moved aside quickly)
Example 2 : That’s just a dodge to get out of paying taxes. (noun: trick)

Pinch :
1 - To press something tightly between the thumb and finger (or two surfaces).
2 - To cause pain by squeezing the skin.
3 - (Informal, British) To steal something.
4 - To reduce in size or tighten by pressing.
5 - A small amount of something, especially powder or salt, that you can hold between your fingers.
6 - A painful squeeze of the skin.
7 - A difficult situation (informal).
Very common in both literal and figurative senses.
“A pinch of salt/sugar” is especially frequent in cooking.
“Pinch = steal” is more common in British English.
Example 1 : She pinched his arm playfully.
Example 2 : My new shoes are pinching my toes.
Example 3 : Someone pinched my wallet on the bus.
Example 4 : Add a pinch of salt to the soup.
Example 5 : During the war, people really felt the pinch.

Mutiny :
1 - An open rebellion against authority, especially by soldiers or sailors against their commanding officers.
2 - More generally, a revolt or uprising against established authority.
Not very common in daily conversation.
Frequently seen in historical, military, or naval contexts.
Sometimes used metaphorically to describe rebellion against leadership in workplaces or groups.
Example 1 : The sailors staged a mutiny and took control of the ship.
Example 2 :
Revolt :
1 - To rise in rebellion against authority or government (verb).
2 - An attempt to end the authority of a person, government, or ruler through protest or violence (noun).
3 - Can also mean a strong feeling of disgust or rejection.
More common than mutiny.
Often used in history, politics, and journalism.
Also used in daily language metaphorically (e.g., "Her stomach revolted at the smell").
Example 1 : The peasants began a revolt against the king’s harsh taxes.

Lance :

2 - Medical tool – a sharp instrument used for cutting open the skin (e.g., to lance a boil).

As a weapon, it’s mostly historical/archaic (medieval knights).
As a verb (cutting open), still used in modern English, especially in medical contexts.
As a metaphor (“lance through the darkness”), sometimes used in literature.

Example 2 : The doctor had to lance the wound to release the pus.

1 - Extremely large or great in size, extent, or importance.
2 - Impressively huge or massive.
Less common in everyday speech, but common in literature, journalism, and formal writing when emphasizing something very big or important.
Example 1 :  And two others in that colossal goatfuck out in the desert.
Example 2 : The ancient statue was of colossal size.
Cordial :
1 - Warm and friendly in attitude.
2 - Sincere and heartfelt.
3 - (Less common) A sweet liqueur or fruit-flavored drink (mainly British English).
Moderately common in formal English when describing politeness or warmth.
The drink meaning is less common, mostly regional (UK).
Example 1 : They received a cordial welcome at the hotel.
Example 2 : She gave him a cordial smile during the meeting.
Tiedown :
1 - A strap, rope, chain, or similar device used to fasten or secure something so it doesn’t move.
2 - (Figurative, less common) A restriction or obligation that limits someone’s freedom.
Common in practical/technical contexts (transport, camping, construction).
Less common in figurative everyday use.
Example 1 : One of those tiedowns worked lose.

Example 3 :
Van :
1 - A medium-sized road vehicle used for transporting goods or people, larger than a car but smaller than a truck.
2 - (In the U.S.) Often refers to a family vehicle with several seats, also called a minivan.
3 - (Old-fashioned/rare) The front line of an army or movement (short form of “vanguard”).
Very common in everyday English when talking about vehicles.
The old-fashioned “army front” meaning is rare and mostly seen in literature or history.
Example 1 : The delivery company uses vans to transport packages.
Example 2 : Couldn't you all of took a van out there?

Example 4 :
Immobile :
1 - Not moving; motionless.
2 - Unable to move or be moved.
3 - Figuratively, not changing or developing.
Common in formal and descriptive English, especially in medical, physical, or literary contexts.
In everyday speech, people more often say “not moving” or “stuck.”
Example 1 : He lay immobile on the ground after the accident.

Example 4 :
Weld :
1 - As a verb: To join two pieces of metal together by heating them until they fuse.
2 - As a noun: The joint or seam made by welding.
3 - Figuratively: To unite or combine closely and inseparably.
Literal welding meaning: Common in industrial, construction, and engineering contexts.
Figurative meaning: Less common, used in literature or formal speech.
Example 1 : The workers welded the steel beams together.
Example 2 : Any of it. If I can be welded, I can weld it.
Example 3 :
Example 4 :
Braze :
1 - As a verb: To join two pieces of metal together by melting and flowing a filler metal into the joint, which has a lower melting point than the workpieces.
2 - Similar to welding, but the metals being joined do not melt.
3 - Figuratively (rare): To bond or unite firmly.
Literal meaning: Uncommon in everyday English; common in metalworking, plumbing, and engineering contexts.
Figurative meaning: Rare.
Example 1 : The plumber brazed the copper pipes together.
Peculiar :
1 - Strange, unusual, or odd.
2 - Belonging specifically to someone or something; distinctive or unique.
Common in both everyday and formal English, especially when describing unusual traits or unique characteristics.
Example 1 : He's a peculiar man. Might even say he has principles.
Example 2 : He has a peculiar way of speaking that makes him memorable.
Example 3 :
Comer :
1 - A person or animal that eats.
2 - Figuratively: A person who is likely to succeed or rise quickly (often used in the phrase “dark horse/comer”).
Literal meaning (person who eats): Uncommon in modern English.
Figurative meaning (up-and-coming person): Moderately common, especially in journalism or sports.
Example 1 : He can take all comers.
Example 2 : The dog is a fast comer when it comes to food.
Maul :
1 - As a verb: To attack or injure someone or something violently.
2 - To handle or use roughly, often causing damage.
3 - As a noun: A heavy hammer or mallet used for pounding.
Verb meaning (attack/rough handling): Moderately common in literature, journalism, and informal speech.
Noun meaning (hammer): Less common, mainly in technical or carpentry contexts.
Example 1 : The bear mauled the hiker before help arrived.
Example 2 :
Truss :
1 - As a noun: A framework, often of beams or bars, designed to support a structure such as a roof or bridge.
2 - A medical device (belt or bandage) used to support a hernia.
3 - As a verb: To tie, bind, or support something tightly.
Structural meaning (framework): Common in engineering and construction.
Medical meaning: Less common, mainly in medical contexts.
Verb meaning (tie/bind): Moderately common, especially in cooking or formal writing.
Example 1 : The engineers designed a strong truss for the bridge.
Example 2 : He wore a truss to manage his hernia.
Hernia :
1 - A medical condition in which an organ or tissue pushes through a weak spot in the surrounding muscle or connective t
2 - Common types include inguinal, umbilical, and hiatal hernias.
Common in medical contexts, less so in everyday conversation unless someone has the condition.
Example 1 : He had surgery to repair a hernia in his groin.

Groin :
1 - The area of the body where the abdomen meets the inner thigh on either side of the pelvis.
2 - (Engineering/Coastal) A low wall or barrier built out into the sea from a beach to prevent erosion.
Body part meaning: Common in medical, sports, and everyday contexts.
Coastal engineering meaning: Less common, mainly in technical contexts.
Example 1 : He felt pain in his groin after playing football.
Example 2 : The swimmer injured his groin while diving.
Thrash :
1 - As a verb: To hit or beat someone or something violently.
2 - To move about violently or violently toss.
3 - In sports or competition: To defeat someone decisively.
4 - As a noun (less common): A violent beating or defeat.
Verb meaning (hit/move violently): Moderately common in informal speech and writing.
Sports/competition meaning: Common in casual sports commentary.
Noun meaning: Less common, mostly literary or informal.
Example 1 : The boxer thrashed his opponent in the ring.
Example 2 : The fish thrashed wildly when taken out of the water.

Example 5 :
Glance-shot :
1 - In general English: a shot (like from a bullet, ball, or object) that hits a surface at an angle and bounces off rather than hitting directly.
2 - In sports (especially cricket, snooker, pool): a light strike that redirects the ball rather than hitting it full on.
3 - In firearms/ballistics: when a bullet strikes a surface at a slant, losing power and deflecting.
Not common in everyday English. More specific to sports, military, and technical descriptions.
Example 1 : The soldier was lucky; the bullet was only a glance-shot off his helmet.
Example 2 : In cricket, he scored with a neat glance-shot to the boundary.
Ricochet :
1 - (Verb) When a bullet, stone, or other projectile bounces off a surface after hitting it at an angle.
2 - (Noun) The act or result of such a rebound.
Not very common in everyday conversation. More often seen in movies, military/law enforcement, video games, or action descriptions.
Example 1 : The bullet ricocheted off the wall and hit the ground.
Steer :
1 - (Verb) To control the direction of a vehicle, boat, or animal.
2 - (Verb) To guide or influence the course of an action, decision, or person.
3 - (Noun) A young male cow that has been castrated, raised for beef.
Very common as a verb (controlling or guiding).
As a noun (meaning a young male cow), it is less common and mostly used in farming or ranching contexts.
Example 1 : He steered the car carefully around the corner.
Example 2 : The teacher tried to steer the conversation back to the main topic.
Example 3 : Course they slaughter steers a lot different these days.
Example 4 : He gripped the steering wheel when the car began to slide.

Pre-vision :
1 - The ability to see or sense something before it actually happens; foresight.
2 - A vision, dream, or mental picture of a future event.
3 - In general use, it can mean planning or imagining something in advance.
Not common in everyday English.
More often seen in spiritual, philosophical, or literary contexts.
In modern English, words like foresight, premonition, or prediction are more common.
Example 1 : Three years ago I pre-visioned it.

Example 3 :
Painstakingly: This describes doing something with extreme care and great attention to detail. If a building is "painstakingly restored," it means every tiny part was repaired very carefully to ensure it looks perfect.
This beautiful old building has been painstakingly restored to its former glory.
Mast (Half-mast): In this context, a mast is the tall pole used for flying a flag. When flags fly at "half-mast" (lowered halfway down the pole), it is a traditional symbol of respect or mourning after someone important has died, such as the King mentioned in your text.
One the day of the King's funeral, flags flew at half mast on all public buildings.
Burglars: These are people who break into a building (like a house or office) illegally, usually with the intention of stealing something.
The burglars gained entry to the building through the back door.
Renovated: This means to repair and improve a building so that it is in good condition again. While "restore" usually means making something look like it did in the past, "renovate" often involves making it look modern or new inside.
The interior of the hotel has been extensively renovated, but the outward appearance of the building hasn’t changed for over 100 years.
